On the treatment of wounds.
I have already stated in the introduction to the treatment of wounds what is required for this section, and now I will explain the treatment of wounds with remedies as follows: [translation note: The word "libre" here likely refers to the title or heading of the current text on wound treatment].
I say that wounds are diverse in their nature, depending on the cause of the wound and the location of the wound on the body.
And the causes by which wounds are often made include: stone throwing, sword or knife cuts, spear stabs, blows from a stick or a club, arrow wounds, and similar injuries caused by various factors.
But wounds that are in different parts of the body include wounds on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, groin, and similar areas on various limbs.
And I will now describe the treatment of certain wounds, which you can use as a guideline for treating other similar types of wounds. [translation note: The word "autras" here means "other". It's not clear what specific types of wounds the author is referring to, but they are likely common or straightforward injuries that don't require special attention].
I will begin with the treatment of simple wounds to the head, as the discussion of more complex injuries will be presented at the beginning of the third chapter. [translation note: The word "simplas" here means "simple". It is likely that the author is distinguishing between simple and complex wounds, perhaps based on severity or location].
Therefore, I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and it is not a fracture, you should consider the following: If it was caused by a stone or similar object, and only the skin has been cut, but the wound is large and there is concern about the development of an abscess in the wound, then you should promptly perform a venesection on the cephalic vein and do not delay. After extracting as much blood as necessary based on the patient's vitality, unless there is another reason preventing venesection, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is scant. [translation note: The term "caut" in this context refers to an abscess or a collection of pus].
And the venesection should be performed on the opposite side of the wounded area, and the patient should avoid overeating, alcohol, and heavy foods.
But if there is any reason to avoid venesection, then reduce the amount of food and avoid meat and wine. [translation note: The term "minuicio" in this context likely refers to a medical or surgical procedure that requires caution].
And if an abscess forms in the wound, place a compress made of wool soaked in oil or wine that has been boiled with styptic herbs on it. [translation note: The term "stipticat" refers to a substance that promotes clotting and helps stop bleeding].
But if there is no sign of an abscess forming, then place a powder made from crushed herbs on the wound while it is still fresh and bleeding, and before the air has had a chance to change its condition. [translation note: The term "recent" here likely refers to a wound that is still actively bleeding or has not yet begun to heal].
But if the air has already changed the condition of the wound, then apply various types of ointments until it heals. [translation note: The term "alscus" here is likely a variation on the word "alcuns", which means "various" or "different". It is possible that the author is suggesting the use of multiple types of ointments, or perhaps simply leaving open the possibility for the practitioner to choose from a variety of options].
But if the wound is large or caused by a sword or similar object and the edges are not close together due to being cut, then bring the edges together with stitches as I will recommend in the section on stitching. [translation note: The term "sutura" refers to the act of sewing or stitching two sides of a wound together to facilitate healing].
But if the wound has exposed bone and tendon and is only held together by a small ligament, then cut the ligament and cure the wound with remedies, restoring the flesh in place of skin, and instruct the patient to avoid heavy or difficult-to-digest foods such as meat and beans until it heals. [translation note: The term "discuberta" here means "exposed". It is likely that the author is referring to a wound that has opened up to reveal underlying tissues].
And if you find putrefaction in the skin that is not attached to the bone, then cut away all of the affected tissue and cure it. [translation note: The term "atrobas" here means "find". It is possible that the author is referring to necrotic or dead tissue that needs to be removed].[
But if an artery or vein is present in these wounds and there is a risk of significant bleeding due to the failure to properly cut it, then you should locate the artery and, if uncut, carefully cut it with a scalpel or tie it off using a ligature. If necessary, cauterize the area to stop the bleeding. [translation note: The term "comfereys" here likely refers to the use of styptic herbs or substances that promote clotting and help stop bleeding].
But if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is likely minor, and you can simply apply this powder to the wound before the air has a chance to change its condition. This will help promote healing and prevent infection. [translation note: The term "denant" here means "before". It is possible that the author is emphasizing the importance of applying the powder quickly, before the wound has had a chance to become contaminated with dirt or bacteria].
The method for making the powder is as follows: combine one part oil of roses, two parts dragon's blood resin, and three parts unburnt alum, then grind the mixture to a fine consistency and apply it to the wound, covering it with a tight bandage. Allow the powder to clot and adhere to the wound before removing the bandage.
Then tighten the bandage above with pads and leave it in place until the wound is no longer at risk of developing an abscess or other infection. This will ensure that the medicine remains in contact with the wound long enough to promote healing and prevent further damage.
Sometimes it is necessary to use only hot cautery when oil of roses and dragon's blood resin are not available.
This method can also be used in the treatment of large wounds that require tight bandages and have not yet begun to heal or become infected. By applying the powder directly to the wound while it is still fresh and before any infection has set in, the practitioner can help promote healing and reduce the risk of complications.
But if the air has already altered the condition of the wound, especially during summertime, then it is not appropriate to use this powder. Instead, treat the wound with the methods previously described, such as using various ointments or applying a poultice made from barley flour mixed with water and honey until pus forms. Then continue treatment with other remedies until the wound heals completely.
But if the wound is a fracture in the bone and it is small, then you can extract it with gentle traction.

Of the instrument, its shape is as follows:
One end of it is curved and sharp, while the other end is not sharp, and its tip is fine but not as thin as a spatula, rather it should be slightly blunt.
And so when the wound is large and some part of the intestines has protruded through it, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural shape and location, if possible in its proper place, because this is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then it is necessary for you to make the patient lie on their side, and place their upper hips higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should place their head and upper legs higher than the lower parts.

And in small wounds, place what is prepared for you, and ensure that the assistant is between your hands, holding the wound with their whole hand and adjusting its lips, and then revealing any area where the suture may have come loose.
And this is the common method of suturing: one should take a needle or the size of the needle according to the size of the wound; and then place it at the end of the wound near the ring finger, pulling the needle with one's fingers without piercing both ends of the thread before making it penetrate the skin inside. And once two ends of the thread and two ends of the suture have been brought together, do this four times.
And then tie the thread double around the needle, and some stitches from both sides together, and then bring the two lips and wound together in an orderly manner; and then hold one end of the thread with the thumb and insert another needle into it, and then tie that one with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not stop doing this with the needles until you have completed suturing the entire wound.
And the needles should be of medium size between thickness and fineness, because fine needles lightly penetrate the flesh, while thick ones are difficult to insert into the thread, so it is necessary that they be medium in terms of thickness and fineness.
And it is not necessary for the needle to be inserted too close to both ends of the thread so that the flesh is not lightly penetrated and the wound opens before its healing.
And do not prolong suturing so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
And then insert the ends of the needles so that the patient does not lose them during sleep; and place powder around the suture from all sides, which will hold the needle ends in place; and leave those until you are sure that the wound is healed.
And this type of suturing with needles is more suitable for small wounds, because even in stitching a single or two stitches, it can be enough.
But the second method of suturing is more common: and it is to stitch the four ends of the thread and two ends of the suture together in one stitch with a needle wrapped in fine and thick thread.
And then, when you have penetrated these four ends with the needle, reduce it from that same side where you started, so that the thread pulls out from the upper part of the wound towards the surface, making the stitch similar to a seam used for sewing fabric together.
And place between each stitch the amount equivalent to the width of the little finger.
This method of suturing is common among people.

On the care of sinuses and the quality of using enema with medicines.

Treatment for paronychia, nail abrasions and incisions, and finger amputations.
Paronychia is an abscess that forms below the nail of a finger or toe and can cause pus to accumulate, leading to infection and swelling. If left untreated, paronychia can become chronic, causing further infection and tissue damage. In severe cases, the infection can spread to the bone, causing it to become necrotic and leading to the loss of the affected digit. The skin may also become discolored or blackened as a result of the infection, indicating tissue death. Treatment for paronychia typically involves antibiotics, wound cleaning, and drainage to remove any accumulated pus or debris. In severe cases, surgical intervention may be required to remove infected tissue or promote healing.[7](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5863167/)
And so when you have that abscess as we have described in the division and it does not respond to your treatment, then it is appropriate for you to incise with an iron all of the excess skin that has formed from the nail and cauterize the wound with it, because cautery is very beneficial in this case.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy, and the additional skin from the nail has already begun to invade the surrounding flesh, causing it to puncture and damage that flesh, then it is appropriate for you to make a fine incision just below the angle of the nail where it is piercing the flesh, and gently push that up and make an incision in the flesh with ease, and place on top of the remaining flesh corrosive burning medications until it has all matured; and then cure it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already invaded the bone, then it is necessary for you to incise the bone and extract it, because there is no time in which it will heal if the bone is corrupt.
And if you see corruption and decay going through the said place, then you should cut into it one of its nodes, according to the location indicated by its reminder (symptoms), and then cure it so that it may be healed.
But if a percussion or friction is applied to the nail and intense pain results, then it is necessary that you first perform bloodletting on the patient, and then dry out the nail with a sharp spatula using a transverse section from top to bottom, ensuring that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, as this would cause the patient to experience intense pain, and could lead to the formation of excessively tight skin in the area. Then cure the affected area with something that alleviates pain.
But tight skin, which is common in older men, can be entirely flesh, or it can be entirely bone, or it can be in the nail.

And with regard to those that are fleshy, cutting is light, and one should make an incision in it at the site of its symptoms using a wide spatula.
But with regard to those that are born on the skin at the joint, healing is difficult, and therefore one should be cautious about cutting it (the corn).
But with regard to one that is born in the nodes of the toe, it is necessary to make an incision at the beginning in the flesh of it using a rounded cut around the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for that purpose, and then cure the affected area so that it may be healed.
But when there is consolidation (scar tissue) that occurs between the fingers due to swelling or by birth in a person who was born that way, or due to consolidation of a wound or burn, then it is necessary to cut into the scar tissue until the fingers return to their natural shape.
And then place between their bandages soaked in scented oil to prevent quick scarring, and separate them (the fingers) or place a thin sheet of lead between them until they heal as necessary.
And similarly, if there is scar tissue on the palm of the hand, then one should dry out that scar tissue according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Regarding incisions for ulcers, they are varicose veins.
Ulcers are varicose veins, which are large veins filled with superfluous melancholic fluids that come in the joints of the limbs, and their most common occurrence is in the legs, especially among travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer purging of the body with black bile several times using strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the treatment of ulcers with iron is done in two ways: one is that it is incised and the black blood is drawn out, and the other way is that the vein is raised and extracted entirely.
The incision is done in this way: first, soak the leg with hot water to soften the thick blood that is in the calf, then bind the patient's leg to the upper part of the thigh using a bandage, and then dry out the vein in one or two or three places using a wide incision, and then raise the black blood using the little finger of the hand on the lower part of the calf, moving it upwards from top to bottom until you have extracted the desired amount of blood that you deem necessary, based on your assessment of the patient's condition.
And then apply that (remedy), and command the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment with bloodletting when the veins are full, and this will be evident to the patient.
But the elevation of ulcers is done in this way: you should examine the leg of the patient and if there are blisters on it, then place them in a bath and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins become swollen; or have them exercise vigorously if a bath is not available until the limb becomes heated.
And then dry out the swelling next to the vein using a long incision in the middle or at the back of it near the ankle, or using a short incision near the heel.
And then open up the swelling using hooks and remove the vein entirely from all sides until it can be felt, and this should be done according to the appearance of the swelling, which will indicate its darkening (towards black).
And then as it (the swelling) comes out and you see that it is white, then it is sinew (tendon).
And then place the kidney stone below it (the incision) so that it is raised and separated from the swelling, and suspend it using an ox leather strap.
And then dry out another incision next to that incision by three fingers' width, and then remove the swelling from above the vein until it is visible.
And then raise it (the vein) and make an incision in the back of the heel, and then extract it and raise it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the upper part of that incision.
And do this until you extract the part of the vein that is higher or above all the incisions, until it is entirely removed, and then make an incision there.
And if it does not obey (your attempts) to extract and raise it, then insert a needle with double the amount of bile and tie it there, and then extract it; or insert below it the stone and rotate your hand around it until it is expelled. And be careful not to make an incision there, for if it is torn, then it becomes difficult to raise it (the vein) and it can cause harm to the patient due to its damage.
And when you have entirely removed it (the vein), then place over the site of the wound a piece of wool soaked in wine and scented oil or unscented oil, and cure the affected area until it heals.
And if the vein is contracted and twisted with a winding around its parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you cut across all of its twists and bends, and suspend it using hooks until you raise it entirely.
And you must be careful with your incision that you do not cut the vein or damage it, for it will be difficult to raise it, and use your prudence in this matter.
Method for cutting a vein:
It is not as sharp as hooks, nor does it have a pointed tip so that it does not damage the vein, and it has thick flexibility like an ox, for if it were too thin, it would cut the vein due to its thinness; it has unnecessary thickness as we have said.
Regarding the extraction of the leg vein:
This vein is born in the legs, in hot lands such as Egypt in the land of Arabs and in the lands of India, which are hot and dry with little vegetation or water.
And it is also easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is due to putrefaction that comes from below the kidneys, similar to the venom of snakes and worms that enter the body, generating between the kidneys and the flesh.
And the sign of its onset is the inflammation of this vein in the leg, accompanied by a vehement burning sensation; and the area becomes swollen, and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of the swelling, resembling the shape of a plant.
And so when the limb appears, it is necessary that you wrap around it a lead weight of one or two drachmas and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as often as there is any cause for it to move outside (of the affected area), cover it again with lead and tie it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut a part of it and wrap the remaining part; and do not cut that which is attached until it has come out completely, because if you cut that which is attached it will contract and enter into the flesh and cause abscess and putrefaction in the area and bad ulceration.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it has been completely removed from the body and there remains no cause of it in the body.
And from this vein, it comes out in some men that is as long as five palms and has already reached me that one came out of twenty palms (length).
And if it is cut during the treatment, then insert the probe into the opening of it and pierce it with a long incision into the flesh, until all that is in the matter has been evacuated, and administer putrefaction of the area for several days with medicines; and treat it with care for the cure of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches and is more common when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and comes to it with many openings, and is emitted from each opening as a branch.
And therefore cure that one as we have said in the division and with what has been said before.
On the incision of worms generated under the skin and it is called cattle disease.
This disease is called in some lands "maladia bovina" because it often affects cows and is a worm that is barely visible, which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes up and down throughout the body and is manifested by sensation from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And the generation of it is due to the putrefaction of some humors, just like how worms and snakes and earthworms and roundworms are generated in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice because when it goes into the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, then easily it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens often.
And therefore when you want to cure it and extract it, then do not proceed unless the sensation of its movement and appearance is clear, and then it is necessary that you strongly hold what is above it and gently but firmly constrict what is below it, and place a drying substance on it and then extract it.

On the incision of disease nafir.
This disease is called "nafir" in our land, and it is pain that comes in some members and often spreads from member to member.
And I have already seen this as you have recommended. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I discovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the "vena funis" (probably referring to a superficial vein) of the arm. After an hour, I saw this inflammation move like a worm crawling upwards, but more gently than it would be if it were alive, like silver flowing from place to place.
And then the pain moved from that place and settled into the muscle, and it stayed there for an hour.
And then it moved into the other body until it reached the other arm.
And it was reported to me that it traveled throughout the patient's body as I saw, and it was remarkable for its movement and speed from member to member. And I had already seen before this in my own experience, but I have also seen many who found that pain moved from member to member. But I have not seen this with feet as I did with the woman.
And I did not think it was anything but that it is because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in her case the sensation of gas moving from place to place was apparent.
It is necessary that this condition does not appear according to this treatment in those who are delicate and with moist veins and weakened bodies.
And if you want to cure this condition and the patient feels the pain, then do not appear at the eye as we have said, but instead apply pressure on it gently and hold a drying substance over it until the trapped gas is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you can cure this condition by inducing vomiting of the body and expelling gas, and using foul-smelling remedies such as pills made from serapium and similar substances.
On the removal of arrows (or "sagittae", a Latin term for arrows)
Arrows are diverse according to their types and the location of the body where they pierce, as their diversity consists of those that have large tips, small tips, concave tips, blunt tips, solid tips, triangular tips, those with tongues, and those with feathers (or "astelas").
And the signs of injury to the brain are when an arrow pierces it and passes through the bone, exiting at the skullcap that is above the brain, causing sudden pain, loss of vision in one or both eyes, dizziness, headache, inflammation and pain in the tongue, muscle spasms, confusion, vomiting bile, difficulty speaking or slurred speech, and white fluid resembling dust coming out of the wound, as well as fluids similar to water from the flesh.
And so if you notice these signs, then you should refrain from treating and removing the arrow, unless it is outside the body.
But the signs of an arrow when it enters the heart, when it is close to the left nipple, and feels as if it has struck a hard object, and does not easily come out; and very light movement of the arrow resembles the pulse's movement, and there is dark blood flow, and swelling with cold sweat, and shivering, and fainting; and so you should know that death is imminent.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are foamy blood coming out of the wound, and swelling in the veins that are near the throat, and a change in the patient's color, and they exhale great spirit, and ask for cold air to be brought.[* I am not a medical professional, so please consult one if you need medical advice.*]
And if the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the foot, and pain occurs near the lower ribs, and a large wound with intense pain and sighing occurs, and not all of the limb's muscles are affected.

And if the arrow strikes the belly and is embedded in it, then there will be a wound or torn tissue or ruptured hernia, and there is no hope for healing by removing the arrow. [* I am not a medical professional, so please consult one if you need medical advice.*]
And if the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out of it, and some substance protrudes from it outside, and there is light pain on the patient, then you should know that it is fatal. [* I am not a medical professional, so please consult one if you need medical advice.*]
But other parts of the body such as the face, neck, shoulder, and scapula, spine, clavicle, hip, thigh, and their similar limbs are safe according to the greater likelihood when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not venomous.

Therefore, this method of practicing medicine has become rare among scholars in our land; and I have not forgotten to include it, as it is essential; nor have I relied on recent works alone, but through extensive reading of the ancient texts and dedicated study of their teachings, I have extracted the knowledge contained within them.
And I have also directed myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
I have described in this chapter all that my knowledge entails and on which I have conducted experiments; as I have summarized it for you and released myself from the burden of lengthy elaboration, I will keep it brief and present a summary of various tools used in this method, as I have done in two previous chapters through added clarification.
Chapter One.
"Summary and collections of what is needed before we begin the recollection of fractured bones and various things to know about each one." "Therefore, we must first speak in this chapter's introduction the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters for which understanding is necessary at the beginning for those who practice this craft and are focused on the truth of them; that is you and those who are eager to learn or master this noble art from you."
"And so I say that when a fracture or dislocation or sprain or injury occurs, it is necessary at the beginning to use bloodletting or leeching on the affected area, or any combination of them, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of virtue, or if it happened to an infant, or an old person in decrepitude, or if it is a time of intense heat, or cold."
"Then let him be content with food that is light, made of fresh herbs and the meat of birds and crustaceans; and prohibit heavy meats and filling up on foods and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain there is no danger of abscess, and if there is no fear of effusion of matter to the limb."
"And then return the patient to their original diet, which is customary for them."
"And when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary for the patient to eat nutritious foods that are thick and heavy, in which there is stickiness, such as food made from wheat baked with water, rice, peas, beef, poultry, fresh fish, coarse wine and similar to these, and with this diet, the consolidation of the fracture occurs more easily."
"And know that fractured bones in men who are either very young or old and decrepit, cannot be continuously healed and consolidated according to their nature due to the dryness of their members."
"But nature adjusts and provides for the fractured bone by any means necessary, such as thickening that adheres to it and strengthens it until they are consolidated and united together, until they reach the point of strength and firmness, just like fire at the beginning, before any cause prevents it from performing its function."
"And for this reason, it is necessary to stop the patient from eating heavy and sticky foods, as we have mentioned."
"And know that the types of fractures are different according to the diversity of body parts, for a fracture in the bone of the leg is different from a fracture in the skull, and a fracture in the bone of the chest is different from a fracture in the bone of the back, and similarly all other members are different from each other."
"And I will defeat forgetfulness with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter."
"There are various types of fractures within themselves." "And the types of fractures in bones are differentiated within themselves; for there is arch-shaped fracture, others that occur crosswise, and some are fractured according to the length of the bone. And there are star-shaped fractures, splintered fractures, and those with or without additional pieces, just like a pen when it is prepared to write, and there is a fracture with wound and disruption in the skull, and there is a small fissure fracture."
"And for every type of fracture, there is a specific remedy and pain in healing it, as will be explained in its own place."
"Signs of bone fracture." "And by these signs a bone fracture is recognized: crookedness of the limb, swelling and appearance to touch, and when compressing it with your hand."
"And therefore, when there is no crookedness or swelling visible and the patient does not feel pain when you touch the bone with movement, then it is not a fracture."
"However, it is possible that there may be crookedness or a small fissure, therefore you should not touch or compress the limb with force, but instead pause and apply medicine that is appropriate for the location, as will be remembered at that moment. And let it be tight with subtle compression."
"And know that when the limb is crooked but not split in two or any other thing that does not happen to it atrophy, such as swelling of all parts of its comparison, then you should begin immediately the process of straightening it, provided that there is no danger of abscess."
"And if there is an abscess, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess ceases, and then treat it in any way possible with lightness and skill."
"And know that the healing of a dislocation is easier and quicker than a fracture in which atrophy occurs."
"And strengthen it as will be remembered later."
"But if there is atrophy in the bone, then it is necessary to extend the broken member separated into two parts, whether it is an arm or a leg, using your hand if the limb is small, or with two ropes or one rope and your hand."
"And your position should be such that you hold the limb in its natural shape while the parts of the broken member are extended, then make sure to perform it carefully and skillfully return those additional pieces to their places."
"And be careful with your strength so as not to cause pain or injury to the patient during your operation, and make sure that you align one bone to its companion in the best possible way according to your ability."
"And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch the limb with your hand, and if you see any other cause, correct it and treat it according to your ability."
"And avoid violent extension and compression, just as foolish doctors often cause abscesses by their operation or cause contractions in the limb, as we have seen with the operations of many of them."
"And the quality of the pressure on the limb should be as recommended, for you should know that fractured members are differentiated in their smallness and greatness and in the shape of them."
"And for those that are small, such as the arm and finger and thumb and similar to them, it is necessary that the wrapping be of fine fabric."
"But for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, it is necessary that they be wrapped in a hard and firm manner because the firm wrapping connects the large and strong member in every part with pressure, so that no filth enters into it."
"And begin, pause appropriately with careful alignment, starting from the location of the fracture with three or four wrappings depending on the limb, and apply a little pressure with your hand on the bandage."
"And come towards the upper part of the location of the fracture and apply less pressure than the tightness with which you have strengthened the location of the fracture." "And extend yourself with wrapping of the location of the fracture slightly, and gradually loosen the pressure until you reach a large portion of it."
"And take another bandage and wrap it around the other side of the fracture with wrapping, and bring the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and your operation should be in the tightness of the wrapping and in loosening that as we have said in the first wrapping above."
"And place between the layers of soft wool or fabric that aligns the crookedness of the fracture, if there is any, and if not, do not place anything in it."
"And place another bandage, and align the layers of splints properly, but if there is no swelling or abscess in the location, then place it on top of these wrappings. But if there is swelling or abscess in the location, then leave it alone for several days, and then apply the splints."
"And let the splints be made of hard cane or carefully crafted wood, or from palm branches, and let the width of each splint be according to this form and shape."
"However, it is necessary that the splint placed on the fracture be larger and wider than the other splints by a little, and the length of the splint should be according to the size of the limb. And bind the splints with another bandage according to your first tightness, and tie it above with string properly as we have said about pressure, that is, let your tightness be greater near the fracture and less as you move away from it."
"And it is necessary that the strings be medium in thickness and fineness and made of flax; and if the strings are thick, as I have seen with some foolish doctors using cords of twisted flax for their operation, this is a great error because it does not allow for equal tightness."
"And thin strings are also not appropriate because you cannot achieve the tightness you want with them, and it is necessary that there be less than a finger’s width between splint and splint."
"And if the patient is injured by the ends of the splints at the tightened locations, then place wool or red woollen cloth underneath it so that it does not cause injury."
"Sometimes a fracture occurs with a wound and there is disruption in the skull, I will come to remember it in its own chapter according to its uniqueness."
"And know that not all broken limbs can be aligned with splints on the first day; this is because for large limbs, it is not necessary to place splints immediately but rather after 5 or 7 days or more, depending on your certainty of the occurrence of abscess."
"And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients healed, they were placed on fractures and dislocations and sprains."

"And sift that as it is with the white of an egg, and apply the mixture of it in a thick but not thin or fine layer, and use that."

Chapter III.
On restoring a broken nose.
Know that the nose is not actually broken unless there are two pieces raised up higher than the rest, or one such piece at least, because in this case there are two bones involved. The lower part of the nose is made of cartilage and is not broken, but rather becomes rough, twisted, and swollen. [Original text: "e no es trencada e no accideys a lu sino atricio e tortuositat e sinuitat."]
But if one of the two pieces is broken, then you must insert a small instrument into the nostril and use it to manipulate the fracture from the inside while using your finger to gently press on the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with care, making sure that your manipulation does not cause pain or discomfort to the patient.
If the break is in the upper part of the nose and the instrument cannot reach it, then use a small rod with a blunt end to gently manipulate the fracture into place.
If there are breaks in two parts of the nose, then you should proceed as described above for each break.
And make every effort to restore the nose to its natural shape on the first day of the injury, if at all possible. If not, then wait until the danger of infection has passed before attempting treatment. [Original text: "e sino, lahoras aprop le vii o·l x deves le repaus del apostema caut."]
And insert a wad of cloth into the nostril if there is one break, or two plugs if there are two breaks. The size of each plug should be such that it fills the nasal passage completely.
Some ancient restorers used to place the plugs in boiled water and change them every day, but I do not think this is necessary. Instead, the wad should be wrapped in a piece of egg white-soaked blotting paper with powdered chamomile added, and left in place until the bone has healed and the cartilage has hardened. [Original text: "e layssa la qualgua entro que l’os sia fermat e sia endurazida la cartillage."]
And sometimes it may be better to insert a small feather or rolled cloth instead of a plug, especially if the break is severe and the patient has difficulty breathing through their nose.
And this method is not necessary for you to use; do as you see fit, whether that is using feathers or plugs.
If an infection develops, treat the nose with a compress of resin or wax infused with wine vinegar and rose oil, or with bread soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
But if there is no infection, then apply a compress of chamomile flour and licorice powder mixed with egg white to the outside of the nose, and place a piece of linen over it. Do not cover the nostrils in any way.
But if the bones of the nose are broken or only slightly fractured, then you must cut open the area and remove any fragments with appropriate instruments. Then close the wound and allow it to heal and solidify with appropriate dressings.
If an injury occurs inside the nose, then you must treat it with plugs and insert lead tubes until it is fully healed.
Chapter IV. [start article on tumors]
The restoration of a broken jaw requires careful attention and skill, as the mandible plays a crucial role in speaking, chewing, and breathing. If left untreated, a broken jaw can result in serious complications such as infection, nerve damage, or difficulty eating.
If the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound associated with the fracture, then you should determine whether the break is external only and not separated into two pieces but instead curved inward. In such cases, the fracture can be easily treated.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jaw, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth and gently push the swelling of the fracture inward to the outer parts of the jaw with your other hand from outside. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, insert your right index finger into the patient's mouth and use your other hand from outside to gently push the swelling inward to the outer parts of the jaw. Do this evenly and carefully.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you must apply extension to both sides according to straightness as much as possible in order to achieve alignment.
If there is dislocation of teeth due to the fracture, then you should strengthen those teeth that you hope will remain with gold or silver or lead wire; and place wax over the broken jaw, and a double cloth, and a thick strip of splint made of strong wood or a sole of a shoe the length of the jaw, and bind it on top with bandages as prepared and suitable for application, but do not destroy it.
And command the patient to rest and be still, and provide them with soft foods that can be easily chewed or even consumed as a liquid.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in any way, then you should re-examine and adjust it on the third day; and correct what has been altered and apply a powder of chamomile, egg white, or crushed chickpeas to the wound after removing the wax, and place wool over the dressing.
And once the dressing is properly attached and the alignment of the bones has not been altered, leave it in place until the fracture has healed and become strong, which usually takes three weeks.
And if another abscess should occur and need to be cauterized, then administer as often as necessary during its healing what we have said in the treatment of it until it is healed and the abscess has drained.
And if the fracture is in a festering state, then consider whether it is a separation of the bone or a dislocation, and carefully use what is appropriate among the instruments to remove any such obstructions.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a fleam-botomy according to your need.
And when you have removed those obstructions and there is no longer any pus, then close the wound if it is wide; but if not, apply one of the appropriate emplasters to it and heal the wound, until it has healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Often, when the collarbone is broken at the front end near the muscle, this fracture occurs in one of three ways:
It may be broken, separated, or separated into two parts without causing any obstructions; this is easy to restore. Alternatively, it may cause an abscess, making it difficult to restore. Or the break may be infected with pus. In either of these latter cases, restoration will be more challenging.
And the operation for this type of break, when it is not infected with pus, is to have two assistants present: one holds the fractured collarbone in place and supports it, while the other stretches the neck towards the injured end.
And then guide the fracture with your hands to form it as needed, so that there is neither too much convexity nor too little concavity.
And if more extension is needed, then place a roll of fabric or wool under the patient's neck, making it as large as necessary; extend and lift the collarbone, pressing down on the roll with your hand to guide the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone to its proper position because it has become lodged in the chest, then have the patient raise their head and shoulders, and place a medium-sized cushion under the affected muscle.
And the assistant should press on the muscle to lift the bone out of the chest and into its proper position, while you straighten the fracture and guide it with your hands.
But if you feel pus in the collarbone and it is bad, then you should make an incision over the affected area and carefully remove the pus.
And if the pus is trapped in the bone, then you should make an incision using one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this purpose, since you had placed a retractor under the collarbone to hold it open; and this retractor has the following shape:
And it is similar to a cylinder, with the following concavity: (image of a cylindrical shape) The width of this retractor should be appropriate for your needs, depending on the size of the bone or its narrowness.
But the length of this retractor should be as long as is necessary for the operation, with two ends that are similar in appearance: one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the wound that you have made when removing pus from the bone is wide and there is a risk of abscess, then close the two sides of the wound with stitches.
And if the wound is small or you are concerned about abscess, then fill the wound with bandages and poultices according to the size of the incision.
And if an abscess occurs, then apply a cloth soaked in vinegar, rose oil, and wine over it.
And finally, make sure that the wooden board does not cause harm to the broken bone. Therefore, it is important to check on the patient every day, and when you see that the swelling has gone down or notice that the board has become loose, then adjust it accordingly and tighten the bandages.
Additionally, have the patient sleep on their back with a small cushion under their neck, and place a roll under the patient's back at night to keep their neck elevated, which will help lift the support for the broken collarbone and hold it in its proper position; or tie the arm to the patient's neck.
And do not remove the bandages for at least 12 days, unless there is an infection or abscess in the area.
Additionally, change the plaster if you see that it has become dirty, adjust the tightness to ensure proper healing and restoration of the broken collarbone, and leave it in place until the fracture has healed and fully coagulated.
And in most cases, the bone will be strong and healed within 28 days, although some people may heal more quickly.
Chapter 6.
On the restoration of a broken shoulder.
The shoulder blade (spatula) is cracked on its side, but not completely broken off from the body. [I assume that "tart" means "tard", which in Latin would be "sero" and in English "late". Here it should mean something like "on its side". If I am wrong, please let me know.]
And so when the shoulder blade is broken but not completely separated from the body, and this is only known through touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare your instruments and reduce it (the fracture) in its natural form in every way possible through any means of engineering that you can.
And then place a poultice of flour and egg white, and a lion's paw bandage on the area; also place two layers of cloth soaked in water; and then place a flat wooden board that is slightly larger than the entire shoulder blade or just a little bigger.
But if the wooden board has a concavity that matches some parts of the shoulder blade, then you should guide this concavity with a lion's paw bandage and adjust the height of the wooden board accordingly to match the shape of the bone.
And then secure it with a long, wrapped bandage that provides gentle but firm pressure to hold the wooden board in place and prevent it from moving out of position.
And consider the swelling every day, and if it becomes softer, then re-tie it tighter and adjust the wooden board if it has shifted out of position.
And have the patient lie on their good side.
The shoulder blade is usually restored in 20 to 25 days.
And when this number of days has passed, then you can remove the bandages and be confident that there is no danger in removing them, as the bone has healed properly.
And if there is a problem with the location of the bone and you place your hand on it and feel that there is pus, then you should make an incision in that area and remove the pus, following the same procedure as for abscess prevention in the treatment of a broken collarbone.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
Sometimes, the foot is broken in its middle part and this occurs late, but the ends of it are more likely to be shattered and splintered.
When the middle of the foot is broken, it often tilts towards the inside and causes great pain, difficulty in walking, coughing, fever, sweating, and vomiting blood. This condition can be easily detected because the cavity in the broken bone is not hidden from sensation.
The treatment for this type of foot fracture is to have the patient lie on their back with a cushion under their hips and another between their shoulder blades. Then, press the muscles around the broken bone and adjust the ribcage using the hands from both sides until the shape of the bone returns to its normal state as much as possible. This should be done carefully and skillfully during the operation and adjustment.
And then place a plaster and sponge over the affected area, followed by a thin wooden board of willow or walnut that is lightweight and wrapped in cloth. Be careful during this process to avoid causing further harm to the broken bone. Apply bandages around the foot, making sure they are not too tight, but still provide adequate support. Check the bandages frequently and adjust as needed, especially if they become loose due to swelling.
If the need arises for releasing the bandage due to incision, pus drainage, pain, or abscess in the area, then remove and loosen the bandages, clear away any damaged plaster, and address the issue using appropriate treatment methods; then reapply the plaster if necessary, making sure it is properly aligned with the wound; and finally adjust the tightness of the bandage as needed until the injury has healed.

Chapter 10.
Of restoring the width (of the spine).
The bones of the spine often break, and if they do, then the fracture is not simply a break in the bone but rather a communication between its ends that extends along the length and inclines towards the interior parts; this results in pain at the site of injury, as well as numbness or paralysis when the fracture occurs.
And the restoration of this condition is to place your hand over the site of injury, aligning it with the shape of the spine as best you can.
And if the fracture is only at the end of the spine, then you should align it according to what is appropriate for you, adjusting it so that it matches its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the middle of the spine or has shifted downwards, then you should have the patient lie on their stomach while you prepare for the restoration of the broken bone. But if an abscess forms, then you must cease all attempts to align or heal the injury until the abscess has subsided, and apply whatever remedy is appropriate for treating it; once the abscess has been dealt with, you may return to aligning and healing the injury as needed.
And if the bone is damaged or shattered, then you must treat it accordingly.
