Chapter 14.
Of treatment for fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is very often broken and it is evident in sensation as it turns to the front or upper part.
And the treatment for it is done by strengthening the ligament over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and having the patient lie on their stomach and face.
And the assistant should extend all the ligaments to their parts according to their quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the knee of the thigh, then the ligament should be placed near the condyle at the tibial side, so that extension occurs in that area, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the break is close to the condyle, then the ligament should be placed near the condyle, and the extension should be in the lower parts.
And the doctor should align the bone with both of their hands so that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined together properly; then apply a plaster and bandage, unless there is danger of abscess in the member. But if abscess occurs in it, then you should leave it alone for several days until the danger from the abscess has passed, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied in this way: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, folding it two or three times, and removing excess material; then wrap the thigh so that it reaches the shin and knee of the thigh.
And insert long wool between the thigh and the bandage near the knee towards the lower parts, so that the ends of the wool come out from the higher part on both sides; then wrap the thigh and leg with it, covering any excess material from the bandage.
And then place seals on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one seal over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the empty spaces between the thigh and leg with soft cloths, so that the bandage is tight.
And then begin the bandage in the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as you move away from the ligament at the site of the fracture, make the bandage lighter, softer, and less tight.
And then extend the wool to both ends towards the lower parts until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the seals on the other side so that the ligament is not displaced from its position.
And then leave the bandage like that, so that there is no danger of corrosion, abscess from cauterization, or inflammation, similar to those; and if any of those occurs, be careful to remove it immediately, and correct what happened as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone that has become loose and fallen off, then it is necessary to heal it as possible; and if not, remove it and leave it, and cure it with what was mentioned earlier until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh bone is strengthened with seals in such a way that they fit well, as we have said for the arm and forearm.
But in the treatment of it done in this way, the patient does not develop a limp; but if it heals on its own and there is no need for application of the other leg, then it is necessary that they walk with a limp temporarily.
And know that the thigh bone heals in 40 days or a little more or less, and all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the treatment of a fracture of the knee cap.
Know that a fracture of the knee cap takes a long time to heal, but it often results in severe stiffness.
But if there is a fracture, then it is not a complete break, but rather a crack or split in its parts, and this can be one or more cracks; and all of this depends on sensation.
And the treatment for it is to heal what has been broken in its parts with the fingers until it is joined and healed as much as possible, depending on ease and convenience of alignment.
And then apply a plaster and place a round seal over it if necessary; and tighten the bandage appropriately.
And then consider all the dispositions that we have mentioned in other fractures, such as abscess from cauterization and similar conditions, so that they can be opposed with appropriate measures until it heals.
Chapter 16.
On the treatment of a fracture of the shin bone.
Know that there are two bones in the shin, one of which is large and is called by the name "shin bone"; and the other is thin and is called the "fibula".
And sometimes both bones of the shin are affected by different types of fractures; and in this case, treatment is done just as for a forearm fracture, and the operation is also similar.
And if both bones are broken together, the shin becomes completely dislocated.
And if only the thin bone is broken, the shin becomes dislocated to the front.
And if only the large bone is broken, it moves downwards; and this is one of those injuries that are not easily healed.
And then administer extension, alignment, and fixation of the fragments according to the healing method.
But if the shin fracture is left open with many wounds, it is necessary that extension be less and more gentle, and relieve it as much as possible with your skill.
And if there is any reason for surgery in the shin, such as when you align the fragments and complete the entire operation, then you will take wooden pegs that are placed below the causes that are placed on the soles, made specifically from palm branches; lighten those with small grooves, but not too heavy, and their length should be according to the length of the shin bone, from the knee to the ankle.
And then wrap each one with two layers of cloth according to its length, and place one on the upper part of the shin from one side and the other on the lower part from the other side, and they should be placed from the knee to the ankle.
And then tie the two fragments in three places at both ends and in the middle, because with this bandage, the shin is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains stationary.
And a splint made of wood should be administered according to the length of the leg, and placed on it the leg to keep it from moving.
And you should do this especially in cases of fracture, and when it is a wound properly speaking.
And you should consider the leg every day and examine it carefully for further study, and protect it from inflammation or other similar causes; and if any of those causes occur, then you should act accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
On the fracture of the ankle and the bones of the foot and toes.
The ankle is not usually fractured as a whole; but the bones of the foot are sometimes fractured; and the toes of the foot are rarely fractured, and they are not prone to fractures except in cases of dislocation.
And if the bones of the foot are fractured and you see those bones raised above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared in this way as if walking.
And then place one foot on something that is raised above those bones, and apply pressure to them with it, pressing and binding them in place until they return to their proper position.
And then apply a poultice and bandage over it, and place a small board under the patient's belly with two rounded ends.
And then strengthen it with the foot in a stretched and supported position, since you have wrapped it with bandages and other necessary materials.
And after three or four days have passed, then you should unwrap the bandages, as you will find the bones bound together, or broken, or dislocated; and the treatment for these is as you know.
But if some toes are broken, then you should reset them and bind them in place as I have taught you in the treatment of finger fractures in the hand. And then place a splint on the broken toe that is as long as the toe, and slightly longer than it; and place this board under the foot, which I have recommended to you, and support it with a proper bandage.
But if two or three or more bones are broken, then you should place a splint of the appropriate size wrapped in linen on each toe; and support the board at the bottom of the foot, and stand one end of the board under the patient's belly so that it remains secure.
And it is important for you to remember not to forget all the causes related to fractures that I have mentioned before, and to respond appropriately to any situation as needed.
Chapter 18.
On the fracture of the hip joint in women, the thigh bone, and the pubic bone in men.
When the hip joint of a woman is broken, then you should place her in a recumbent position with a cushion under her head, and incline her pelvis slightly towards one side, supporting it from behind.
And the midwife should fill the hip joint with cotton, and make it as round as possible; and then the woman should be turned over, and her hips gradually raised until the broken bone returns to its proper position in front of the pelvis, as expected.
And then place a pillow under the woman's hips, and when she needs to urinate, gently remove the cotton and help her do so with ease, and then return the cotton in the same manner as before, and finally return her to her original position. [Note: The translation of "retorne al sieu pulvil primier" is a bit uncertain without more context.]
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, as the hip joint will have healed by then.
And if you wish, take an inflated bladder made from sheep intestines and insert a reed cannula into it; then insert the entire bladder into the hip joint and blow gently into the cannula with strength until the bladder is inflated inside the hip joint, which will help the fracture heal. [Note: This treatment is not recommended in modern medicine.]
And then fill it with cotton and leave it in place for several days, as we have recommended, until it has healed.
And when the thigh bone in a man or woman is broken, then administer the same treatment and reduction as we have recommended for the hip joint.
And what is right is not hidden in these common and infrequent fractures, especially for those who have some knowledge, experience, and understanding of this book; for the treatment of such a fracture involves correct reduction and immobilization, which should be understood well.
But when the pubic bone in a man is broken, then take goose grease and apply it to the area; then wrap the bone with cloth and allow it to remain in place for three days or thereabouts until it has healed.
Chapter 19.
On the treatment of bone fractures when there is a wound present.
It is important for anyone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone or shinbone, to seek immediate surgical treatment as soon as conditions allow, as we have previously discussed.
And if the wound is bleeding profusely, then it is necessary to take steps to control the bleeding by applying powdered egg white or another appropriate substance to the wound, unless there is another cause for the bleeding.
And then begin treating the fracture on the same day, and do not delay unless there is a risk of infection or inflammation. [Note: The translation of "apostema" is uncertain without more context.]
But if an abscess forms, then you should delay treating the fracture for nine days until the infection has subsided, and do not attempt to treat it on the third or fourth day, as this may cause further complications.
But if the broken bone is protruding from the skin and is clearly visible, then it is necessary to reduce the fracture and bind it with bandages as soon as possible, using gentle pressure and minimal extension. [Note: The translation of "codena" is uncertain without more context.]
But if you are not prepared for extension and the reduction of the fracture is difficult, then you can use this instrument made of iron, which is seven or eight fingers long and has a width appropriate for the size of the wound; it is necessary for the physician to have three or four such instruments on hand in order to treat various types of fractures. [Note: The translation of "tieuas mas" is uncertain without more context.]
And the instrument should be round and have a slight curvature so that it does not bend or break during the operation.
And the instrument should have a sharp point with a blunt edge at its tip; it should be thicker in the middle than at the ends, so that it can grip and hold onto the bone securely during reduction. [Note: The translation of "grossetut" is uncertain without more context.]
And this is its shape:
And it is named in Greek "labre voluntas atelem", which means "small palette". [Note: The translation of the Greek term is uncertain, as there is no exact equivalent in modern medical terminology.]
And so it is necessary to place the sharp end of the instrument above the prominent end of the bone, so that it can reduce and stabilize the fracture. [Note: The translation of "eminent" is uncertain without more context.]
And then gently push the instrument into the wound until the bone returns to its proper position, and bind it with appropriate bandages to hold it in place.
And then make an effort to bind the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end is small and cannot be grasped with the instrument, then it is necessary to insert the instrument into the bone itself in order to gain purchase and reduce the fracture.
But if you are unable to reduce the fracture using the methods we have recommended, then it is necessary to use specialized instruments or saws to cut through the bone and facilitate reduction in some way that is possible for you. [Note: The translation of "inscizoris" is uncertain without more context.]
And then remove any rough or jagged bone fragments that remain in the wound, so that they do not interfere with healing or cause further injury.
And when the bone has been reduced, but the patient still experiences intense pain and discomfort, then you should know that the bone is not in its proper position or alignment; and so it is necessary to perform additional reduction or manipulation in order to provide relief and promote healing.
After you have completed the reduction and treatment of the fracture, then apply a clean dressing to the wound, preferably using a black, antiseptic solution such as stypic vi negre; and avoid using any ointments or other substances that contain oil, as this can lead to infection and corruption in the wound. [Note: The translation of "stypic vi negre" is uncertain without more context.]
And then administer splints or other supportive devices as needed during the healing process, and leave the wound uncovered so that it can be easily cleaned and monitored; use sterile dressings and bandages to cover the wound and prevent infection. [Note: The translation of "forfexs" is uncertain without more context.]
And take great care not to apply excessive pressure or tension to the wound and fracture site, as many foolish physicians have done in the past, causing injury, infection, or other complications that can lead to death or disability; instead, use gentle and careful techniques to promote healing and prevent further harm. [Note: The translation of "zuthement" is uncertain without more context.] And if the bandages or splints are loose, soft, and different from those used for other types of fractures.
And if the wound is large or severe, and you are concerned about potential complications that we have discussed, and you experience pain at the site, then it is not advisable to apply splints; instead, use firm dressings and bandages to hold the area in place and prevent further injury. [Note: The translation of "astelas" is uncertain without more context.]
And when the wound begins to heal after one or two days and you notice the formation of pus or other signs of infection, then remove the dressing that you had applied with vinegar, and instead apply warm compresses made from cloths soaked in a solution of tetrafarmacon or another appropriate antiseptic, as is our custom for treating wounds. [Note: The translation of "tetrafarmacon" is uncertain without more context.]
And it is important for you to unwrap the bandages and examine the wound every day morning and evening until it has healed and become firm; and make sure that the limb is positioned in such a way that pus can easily drain from the wound and not accumulate, causing further complications. [Note: The translation of "poyredura" is uncertain without more context.]
And if the wound has not healed and there is still pus present after several days, then you should know that this indicates a deeper infection; in this case, it is necessary to probe the wound with a sterile instrument to assess its depth and determine the best course of treatment. [Note: The translation of "satagias" is uncertain without more context.] And when you find pockets of pus or infection that are separate from the surrounding tissue and can be easily removed, then do so; but if the infection is deep-seated and cannot be easily extracted, then it is necessary to perform a more invasive procedure in order to remove the infected material and promote healing. [Note: The translation of "satagias" is uncertain without more context.]
And if the wound becomes inflamed or necrotic, with signs of infection or putrefaction such as abscesses or ulcers, then it is necessary to apply appropriate treatments such as topical medications, debridement, or other interventions in order to promote healing and prevent further complications. [Note: The translation of "zuchem" and "eluchen" is uncertain without more context.][Reference(s):
And regarding the matter of bone fractures in large bones such as the thigh bone or shinbone, where my words and your actions must align: when a thick bone is broken, it protrudes from the limb like the bones of the hip or knee, and you should not attempt to pull it back into place or extract it, as this can cause death; rather, leave it in place if possible, as it will likely fall back into position on its own within 20-30 days. And then treat the wound if it is visible, but if not, leave it alone.
Capital twenty
On the treatment of swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture wound.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of healing of a fracture, especially if it is close to the joints, and it disfigures the appearance of the limb; and it easily prevents the member from performing its natural function.
Therefore, consider whether the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, dragon's blood, and similar substances; so that you take some or all of them, and combine them with vinegar, albumen from egg whites, or wine, and apply it to the swelling on a piece of cloth, and bind it tightly with good bandages.
And leave the bandages on so that you do not remove them for several days; and then loosen the bandages and apply a new one, until the swelling has subsided; or place a lead plate over it, because lead has the property of healing all inflammation in the limbs.
But if the swelling has turned into a hard and tight stone, and it is necessary to remove it carefully, then incise it along its highest part, and extract the superfluous and protruding parts or cut it with sharp surgical instruments until it has subsided; and care for the wound until it heals.
Capital twenty-one
On the restoration of fractures when it has healed and the member is not functioning properly or moving as it should naturally.
When a fracture of a bone has healed, and the member is not functioning properly or moving as it should naturally, this is caused by several factors: one of which is an excessive amount of dissolution of the ligament and inappropriate ligation; or due to excessive tension of the ligaments, preventing proper nutrition from reaching the member; or due to an excessive amount of ointment applied; or due to movement at an inopportune time; or due to a lack of sufficient blood in the patient's body, and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient well, encourage a healthy appetite and promote blood production in their body; administer baths; and provide entertainment, joy and happiness around them.
And then apply a poultice on the member to draw nutrition to it, or continue applying an ointment with warm water to it, until proper circulation and movement returns to its natural state.
Capital twenty-two
On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal in a crooked manner and prevent them from performing their proper function.
When a healed member has a healing of crookedness and protrusion at the fractured bone, swelling, and the appearance is disfigured, but the member can still perform its natural function, it is not necessary to consider that the member has been newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish doctors and healers who have made this mistake in our land, and this operation is highly reprehensible and leads to great fear, and the sign of it is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then it is appropriate to apply warm water with soothing herbs such as marshmallow root and mallow leaves, and clover, among others; and make a plaster with emollient properties, such as a well-made dough plaster; or take the pith of althea root and mix it with vinegar or camphor oil, and apply it as a plaster; or use ripe figs, triturated with dove dung, among other similar remedies that promote consolidation.
And sometimes it is resolved by gentle and frequent friction with moist hands, and the member should be moved in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has already formed and is severe, becoming hard like stone and requiring surgery for correction, then it is necessary to cut off the part that protrudes, dissolve the continuity of the bone with a saw, and remove any excess swelling from the area with fine incisions. Then make an opening in the bone and provide easy access for surgical instruments and study.
Capital twenty-three
On speech in dislocation
A dislocation is a displacement of one of the joints from its proper location, which prevents movement, distorts the appearance of the limb, and causes pain and great harm to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is important to act quickly and attempt reduction as soon as possible, and not delay the process, because if it is delayed, the area becomes infected and it makes the reduction of the dislocation much more difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or stretch the limb during infection, because it often causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this occurs, it is necessary to proceed with caution and perform bloodletting on the patient, and wait until the infection subsides before attempting reduction; and apply warm compresses to the area and use oil to facilitate movement, and then attempt reduction with care.
And every member should be treated with whatever comes to mind when needed.
And I have already organized the chapters on dislocation as previously described in fracture; from the uppermost part of the body to the lowermost part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two mandibles can become dislocated if not careful; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: either they are slightly removed from their places due to weak muscles, or they are completely dislocated at the joint, causing saliva to flow from the patient's mouth and making it impossible for them to close their mouth or speak clearly, with their tongue unable to move as it should.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete, then gentle reduction must be administered promptly and not delayed.
And it is necessary for the caregiver to hold the patient's head, and for the doctor to insert their thumb of one hand into the space of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or for them to insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, while keeping the other fingers outside the patient's mouth holding onto the cheeks and guiding the jaw. The doctor should then instruct the patient to relax their jaw and release any tension, and gently guide the jaw back into place with their thumb(s).
And if reduction is difficult, especially when both mandibles are dislocated, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil to make the reduction easier and faster, without delaying the reduction of both. This is what we have said.
And so when the mandible returns and is held in place, and the patient's mouth is closed and not slack, then apply warm cloths with wax to the area, using gentle friction, and use soft bandages to secure it in place.
And let the patient sleep on their back with their head resting between two pillows, to prevent any discomfort or pain in the jaw, and do not force them to chew or eat anything until their jaw has healed and regained its strength. Have them eat soft foods that can be easily cut into small pieces with a knife, until their jaw is fully healed.
And this should be administered with ease, without causing difficulty or strain when eating, drinking, or speaking, until the jaw has healed and regained its strength.
And if the reduction of both mandibles is difficult when they are dislocated and have not returned to their proper positions, it is often accompanied by fever, swelling, and persistent pain, and the patient's stomach may become distended or bloated, and they may experience nausea and vomiting pure bile.
And when you see this, you should know that it is a dangerous situation, and many patients who experience these symptoms do not survive more than ten days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of dislocation of the clavicle and the end of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the torso due to its continuous connection with the chest; and in some cases, it may be dislocated on the outside, which can be determined by sensation.
And the restoration of the clavicle is for the patient to lie on their back with their arms extended. And then gently press the area with your palm and apply firm pressure, as this will help it return to its proper position.
And then place a warm compress or poultice on the area and apply gentle traction, holding it in place.
But if the end of the muscle is attached to the bone and has not become dislocated, then it must be reduced and held in place as we have said, using whatever tools or methods are available to you.
And then apply warm compresses or poultices and use traction to help reduce the dislocation, instructing the patient to remain calm and restful until they have fully healed.
And with this medical treatment, the end of the muscle is returned to its location when it has become separated.
Chapter 26. (This is a common way to start a new chapter or section in medieval texts)
On the reduction of a muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated unless it is in one of three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the tendon to the fleshy part. (Titillic could be an old term for tendon)
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens late; and it is not dislocated at the back by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front by the nerves. (Here the text seems to imply that there are other causes for pain in those areas, such as nerve issues)
And most commonly it is dislocated and pushed out at the lower part near the tendon, and this happens more easily in those with little flesh, but in those with a lot of flesh, it is pushed out with difficulty. (Here the text seems to imply that people with less flesh around the muscle are more prone to dislocation)
And it often happens that by a blow or fall, the muscle of some men becomes abscessed with a "caut" abscess, and is thought to be dislocated because of this. (Here the text seems to imply that sometimes what appears to be a dislocation could actually be an abscess)
Therefore, you must first determine if this is the case, and then proceed with the treatment. (Here the text implies that a proper diagnosis should be made before starting any treatment)
And the dislocation of the muscle is recognized when it is at the lower part near the tendon; so that you can distinguish between a dislocated muscle and a healthy one, for you will find clear differences between them; and you will find at the head of the muscle a concavity and depression below the tendon near the touch of the head of the muscle similar to an egg, and the patient is unable to move their hand to their ear or to make any movement with it.
If it is dislocated above or below, you will find a noticeable deformity that is not hidden. (Here the text implies that a clear sign of dislocation is the presence of a visible deformation in the affected area)
And reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent and the patient is a child. (Here the text implies that a fresh dislocation in a child is easier to reduce than an older one)
And the reduction of the dislocation is done by lifting the patient's hand to the upper part, and placing their heel pads just below the tendon, then lifting the joint with force back into its place. The practitioner should then lift the patient's hand and extend it while placing a support beneath it so that it returns gently to its original position. (Here the text describes a technique for reducing a dislocation: by lifting the limb above the muscle, supporting it just below the tendon, and then applying force to move the joint back into place)
But if despite everything we have said, the dislocation does not return to its original position and persists for several days after the initial injury, then it is necessary that the patient bathes in warm water and receives a poultice that softens and lubricates, such as costus root, fenugreek, and melilot in water. This should be administered to help reduce the dislocation.
And let the patient lie on their back with a woolen pad placed just below the tendon, and let the doctor place their knee on the pad to apply pressure, while lifting the head of the muscle with strength, and pulling the patient's hand to prevent it from being injured in the lower part. Another assistant should hold the patient's head to prevent injury to the lower part, for the muscle will soon return to its original position.
And if you wish to reduce it in this way: have another person present who is taller than the patient and stand on their side, then insert their muscle just below the tendon of the patient and lift the tendon upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand downwards from below their belly. But if the patient is lightweight, then it is better to hang something heavy from them to add weight and help the dislocation return quickly to its original position.
And it is also reduced in another way: which is to place a long stick on the ground with a round cap like a mortar pestle that is neither too thick nor too thin, and then place the patient over the cap of the stick. Then lay a lion's cloth over the patient's head and have them stand along the length of the stick, while extending their hand downwards on one side and firmly pulling their body straight on the other side, causing the joint to quickly return to its original position with speed.
And if the reduction of the dislocation is difficult for all the reasons mentioned, then use this treatment: which is to take a stick that is two cubits long and four digits wide, and make its thickness lighter near the tendon in this way:
And over the rounded cap should be placed leather strips covered with lion's cloth, to prevent the stick from injuring the patient.
And the stick should be placed just below the tendon of the patient, and the arm or hand should be extended over the stick towards the lower part, while being secured with straps around the stick, arm, and the end of the hand.
And the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand extended downwards, while the other side of the body is suspended from the other side, for the joint will quickly enter its place.
And once the insertion of it is complete according to each method that is possible for you, then you should place a woolen sphere of even quantity beneath the tendon, and apply a plaster prepared with powdered moly and egg white over the entire muscle, rolling it upwards. You should also strengthen the area around the tendon with moderate tension, and wrap the bandage over the plaster, and attach the patient's hand to their neck, leaving it alone without moving for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be reduced, until the affected limb is comfortable, for it facilitates their recovery.
Then gradually increase the food intake, or after the seventh or fifth day, also engage in exercise; and when it is firm and not swollen, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated due to excessive moisture or for another reason, then it is necessary to apply a pointed cautery with three strokes to that area, as mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
And if all of this is done and after seven days the ligament is still loose, the joint is not closed, and the plaster and bandages have been applied several times without improvement, and the patient cannot lift their arm above their head, then know that the nerves at the muscle's cap have been cut, stretched, or weakened, and understand that the joint will never be able to return to its proper position.
The reduction of a dislocation in the foot or breast area, which is caused by impact and stretching with hands, should be done until it returns to its original position.
And apply another bandage on that area, and take care of it until it is healed.
And if there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement during the healing process, then have the patient take many baths until the heaviness subsides and they return to their natural state.
Chapter 27.
Chapter 27: On the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; it can be dislocated in any direction, but most commonly in front-to-back direction, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is accompanied by a noticeable change or deformation based on the specific way it is dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated hip with your hand, it should be clear to you that the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or touch its own muscle.
And it is necessary to act quickly and reduce the dislocation before pus forms (an abscess), as a dislocation with an abscess is difficult to correct, and it may not heal completely, especially when it occurs in the back direction, which is the worst of all types of dislocations, causing great pain, and often leading to death.
And the restoration of a dislocated hip, when it is possible to correct it, involves extending the patient's arm with two hands, expanding their legs, and placing the doctor's hands on the hip and pushing it back into place using their knuckles or the edge of their palm until it returns to its proper position.
But if the dislocation is in the front direction, then it should be corrected by bending the patient's arm with their own hand until they strike the hip with the edge of their palm.
And if the dislocation does not respond to reduction, then apply strong and forceful extension.
And have two assistants hold the arms and another two maintain that stretching position to prevent re-injury during the extension process.
And then rotate the arms in all directions while it is wrapped with a long folded cloth or bandage over the patient's hand or ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with their hand, apply some oil to it to facilitate lubrication and ease of movement, then push forcefully until it returns to its proper position.
And the reduction of a dislocated hip requires placing it on a plaster containing styptic and exudative properties with egg white, and wrapping it with a bandage to hold it in place, suspending the arm from the neck, and leaving it for several days.
And when the hip joint returns to its proper position, loosen any ligaments and leave them alone.
And if you see that the joint is not getting stronger, reapply the plaster and ligaments, leaving it on for several days until it heals, then loosen them.
But if the patient feels heavy or weak during the reduction of the joint and their position is unstable, then apply moist heat with gentle friction and compression until they feel relief; or place a greasy cushion on the joint to reduce inflammation, and then bind it in place for a day and night. And then apply heat and gently massage the joint with temperate friction, and repeat this process three times, using a mildly warm poultice each time. Corruption has already reached the internal parts of the tooth under the gum line before it is covered with tissue.
If necessary, place a warm and moist cow's bladder on the joint and apply gentle pressure to reduce inflammation. Repeat this several times until the swelling is reduced and the joint returns to its normal position.
Chapter 28.
Chapter 28: Treatment for Dislocation of the Shoulder (the joint between the arm and the hand)
The dislocation of the wrist is often encountered and its reduction is relatively easy, but it is still crucial to act quickly before the area becomes infected or develops pus. This is in contrast to other joints, where the reduction process can be more complicated.
The reduction of a dislocated wrist can be done by placing the patient's hand on a table and having an assistant extend their arm while another person places their palm or hand over the joint and pushes it back into place until it returns to its normal position.
However, it is important to consider the position of the patient's hand before attempting reduction. If the palm faces downward, have them place their back on the table for extension and reduction. If the dislocation is on the back of the hand, position their hand with the palm facing upwards on the table so that your hand can easily reach the joint.
If the dislocation is reduced immediately, that is good. But if not, then apply a yielding plaster to reduce swelling and allow it to heal for several days. Do not attempt reduction again until the swelling has subsided, as attempting too soon can cause further injury and delay healing.
However, if the joint remains dislocated despite attempts to reduce it and the patient experiences weakness or numbness in their hand, it is likely that the nerves have been damaged. In this case, surgery may be necessary to repair the damage and restore function to the limb. This should only be done by a skilled surgeon with experience in treating nerve injuries.
Once the dislocation has been reduced, apply a plaster as recommended and leave it on for five days to promote healing. After that, loosen the bandage and allow the limb to rest for a few days before gradually resuming exercise to restore mobility and strength.
But if the limb is heavy and there is pain or swelling, apply warm water compresses with gentle friction several times until the symptoms improve.
Chapter 29.
Chapter 29: On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
Sometimes, toes can be dislocated in any direction.
When a toe is dislocated on the side of the palm or the sole, then straighten it and push the dislocation back into place using your thumbs until it returns to its normal position.
And then support the head of the toe and suspend it at the end where it is dislocated, leaving it in place for two days.
Then suspend and extend the toe until it is straightened on its own in that day.
And when night comes, bind it as described and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising with movements, and bind it again at night; and do this for several days until it becomes straight.
Perform a similar procedure if the toe is dislocated on the sole of the foot, bind it to that side and follow the first operation until it heals.
Similarly, do the same thing when it is dislocated in other areas.
Chapter 30.
Chapter 30: Treatment for Dislocation of Vertebrae in the Back
When there is a complete dislocation of one of the vertebrae in the back or neck and multiple vertebrae are displaced from their positions, then there is no cure for this condition as it can lead to death for the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion is expelled involuntarily, and they cannot hold it in; and often some members or two sides of their body or both arms are weakened or one of them; or a vertebra has shifted from its place, as often one shifts and often there is a movement of these to four directions.
Therefore, if the dislocation is in the back direction, it is called "gelbozitation".
But the treatment for this condition depends on whether it occurred in childhood or adulthood. If it happened during childhood, then there is no cure or complete healing for this condition.

And place the majority of the table over the vertebrae, and the doctor places their feet on the other end, and pulls strongly so that the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.

Make sure that a hole is drilled through the center of each stick, and securely fasten each one to the ground so that they do not move.

Capital XXI
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle only become dislocated and do not suffer from minor displacement or concavity as other joints do.
And the hip joint can become dislocated in four ways, which are: when it is displaced towards the inside, outside, front, or back.
And it is most commonly dislocated towards the inside, and less frequently in the front or back directions.
And the sign of a dislocation towards the interior is that when you align the healthy leg with the affected leg, the latter appears longer and more prominent, and the patient is unable to bend their knee without pain, and there is swelling in the area where the thigh meets the hip, which indicates an abscess or inflammation caused by a cautery.
And the sign of one who has suffered a dislocation towards the exterior is that the symptoms are contrary to those described above.
And the sign of one who has suffered a dislocation towards the front is that their leg extends normally, but they are unable to bend it at the knee due to pain, and if they try to walk they cannot move their legs forward; their urine is retained and there is swelling caused by an abscess, and they must be carried on a stretcher.
And the sign of one who has suffered a dislocation towards the back is that their knee does not extend normally, and they are unable to bend it at the knee without also bending their thigh; their leg appears shorter than the other leg, and their thigh is soft or flaccid; the head of their femur has moved towards the back.
But the reduction of this type of dislocation is that you consider, therefore, whether the dislocation is old and chronic, in which case it has already hardened, and so you administer reduction according to its condition; but if it is not in this case, then there is no need for any further action.
But in the case of one whose dislocation is recent and is one of the four types described earlier, you should do as follows: return the joint to its proper position and extend it both internally and externally, and then move it to the right and left, as this will quickly restore it to its normal position without the need for any other treatment.
But if it does not return to its normal position using this method, then you should do the following: prepare an assistant who is strong and extend the patient's leg downwards, either with their hands or with a ligature tied above the knee; have another assistant place their hand on top of the leg, just below the thigh, so that they can apply pressure to the muscles; then, using a leather strap, pull firmly on the patient's hip joint and hold it in place while another assistant grasps the free end of the ligature; finally, have the patient flex their other knee and place it against the affected leg, providing additional support.
And the extension should be to the inside, towards the groin, or to the back, towards the spine.
And all of these extensions should be done in one motion, with the patient raised off the ground and suspended.
And this type of swelling is common to all four [extremities].
And so, if the dislocation returns with what we have mentioned, it will be fine; otherwise, it is necessary for each type to do what we will say about its own cure.
In reducing its own dislocation, when it is dislocated to the inner parts, it is necessary for the patient to be reclined on their side; and place the ligaments on the crease of the thigh at that point which lies between the head of the thigh bone and the area beyond the groin; then extend the ligament above the part near the groin to the highest part of the collarbone.
And another strong minister should firmly grasp the patient's arms and press hard on the large muscle of the thigh, extending it outwards forcefully, for this will return to its place.
And this type is of easier cure than the others where this member is reduced.
But if it resists you and does not obey this type of cure completely, then it is necessary to tie both of the patient's legs together firmly with ligaments, lightly, around the ankles and knees.
And let the length of each (ligament) be equal to four fingers, and let the patient's affected leg be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And let the patient be suspended over the top of the post, and (be) in the middle; and let the distance from the ground to them be two arm lengths.
And command a strong young man to bend the head of the thigh, and let them be suspended with the patient along with another young person, and the other young person should forcefully push the thigh to return the joint to its place quickly.
But in reducing its own dislocation, when it is dislocated to the outer part, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their back as we have recommended when they have obeyed; and let the ligaments be tightened specifically on the affected leg and on their chest.
And let two poles be placed, one under their feet and the other near their head; and let adjusted poles be placed in the middle of the raised peak (mound) covered with dampened cloth, so that the patient is not harmed by the pole between their thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not hindered.
And let each minister extend their part, and the doctor with their hand guide the reduction according to art (expertise).
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary for the thigh to be extended slightly, and this should be done as explained earlier on a mound; and let the doctor place the palm of their right hand on the affected patient's groin area, and with the other hand, let them press there to close it, and with that pressure, extend it to the lower parts towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards and should be raised from the ground; it is necessary for them to lie on a hard surface, just as is necessary for the rising (treatment) if the hip is dislocated outwards, as we have said about placing it on two thighs; and let it be on its back with the ligaments extended, as we have said, without delay.
And it is necessary to apply compression with a board on the area where the joint protrudes.
And when the reduction is completed as necessary - and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and this is that the patient extends two thighs, and when you see them aligned and the patient stretches their leg and releases their resistance, then you know that the member has returned as necessary - then adjust the thighs and place a paste and stretch it with bandages so that the hip is not damaged in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then undo the ligaments and paste, and compare one thigh with the other.
And then, if you see them aligned properly, then know that the dislocation is now stable, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of softness, then return and reapply the paste on it, extending it as before, and let it remain for three days.
And then undo that, and delay their walking on it for several days until they have regained strength.
Chapter 32.
Chapter 32: Treatment for Dislocation of the Knee Cap.
The kneecap (denolh) can be dislocated in three ways: either to the outer parts, to the inner parts, or downwards towards the back; it is not dislocated towards the front.
And the sign of a dislocated kneecap (denolh) is that you command the patient to bend their leg and bring it towards their thigh, and if they are unable to do so, then you know that the kneecap is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation of the kneecap (denolh) is as follows: have the patient lie down straight, and if they are able to, extend their leg; place your two hands on it, and place the patient's foot between your legs; then press the kneecap with your palms, holding it firmly between your fingers, while another minister extends the patient's leg until the kneecap returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction of the dislocated kneecap (denolh) is that the thigh adheres to the hip without effort or difficulty.
And then apply a paste on the kneecap (denolh) and bind it to the thigh with bandages for three or four days, then undo the bandages.
And the patient should not walk for several days, until they have healed and regained strength.
But if the reduction of the kneecap (denolh) is difficult for you, then apply strong extension with ligaments, as was described in the treatment of a hip dislocation earlier.
Chapter 33.
Chapter 33: Treatment for Dislocation of the Ankle.
Some times the ankle is swollen due to minor injury, and sometimes it is dislocated based on its position, and the dislocation can occur in the inner or outer parts.
And the sign of the ankle dislocation is that you can see the ankle swollen, pointing towards the direction where it is dislocated.
But the treatment for the swelling of the ankle is simple and it consists of stretching it gently with hands and bandaging it so that it returns to its normal state.
But when treating a dislocation based on its position, it is necessary to lay the patient down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant stand behind their affected side in the middle of their back.
Then you should place your right hand on the higher part of the foot and your left hand on the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards your right hand and hold it with your left hand twice, extending it, and then push the heel of the foot with your right hand towards the thigh without any effort.
And do that twice, as we have mentioned.
Then push the heel of the foot against the thigh for the third time and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two tries following this method and you see the foot bandaged, then it is good. But if not, then you should repeat the operation because it will return (to its normal state).
But if you are prohibited from treating it as we have recommended, then make the patient lie down on their back on the ground, place a solid piece of wood or something similar under them, which fits between their thighs, and wrap a cloth around them so that they do not get hurt.
And have the assistant of the patient hold their thighs and have another assistant stretch the foot with their hands or use a device that goes around the leg of the foot, and let each assistant pull on either side against each other's force, and make sure that the wooden piece is placed between the patient's thighs holding it in place so that the body of the patient does not move towards the lower parts because of the extension.
And let the doctor bandage the dislocation with their hands and another assistant hold the healthy thigh downwards because the dislocation will return easily.
And when the dislocation returns and you are assured of the success of the treatment, then apply a plaster and use bandages strongly to wrap around the foot and leg.
And it is necessary to be careful or avoid the nerves which are behind the ankle, because applying strong bandages over them can harm and hurt them significantly.
And let it remain for two or three days, and if the bandage becomes softer, remove it.
And unwrap it on the third or fourth day and forbid the patient from walking for 40 days. But if it is necessary to walk before that time frame, then there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be re-injured and become corrupt, which may prevent healing from taking place.
And if an abscess forms, then it is necessary to administer in the treatment of that abscess what we have previously mentioned in our narrative, in a place for healing and incision until it is swollen (reduced).
Capital Chapter 34. (This indicates the end of one chapter and the beginning of another.)
Chapter 34: Treatment for Dislocation of Toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated toe be bandaged with a little extension without much effort, and this is not difficult, it is light (to do).
But if the dislocation is in one of the joints of the toe, then it is necessary to make the patient lie down with their foot on a flat surface or table and keep their body still as if they were walking.
And you should stand with your weight on one foot and place the patient's foot on top of yours, right at the joints, and then press it down with your foot using force until it returns (to its normal state), making sure that the area is bandaged and there is no swelling.
Then place a board under the patient's foot that supports the whole foot, with two ends, and hold it firmly with tension for three days; then unwrap it and let the patient rest from walking for many days, until they are healed and recovery is assured.
Capital Chapter 35. (This indicates the end of one chapter and the beginning of another.)
Chapter 35: Types of Dislocation Made by Plague, Fracture or Any Combination.
When any of those causes occur and you attempt to treat them and restore the affected area, many times it results in death.
And so it is not advisable to be presumptuous in treating such cases, but only those who are skilled in the art, with long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash or overconfident; and who in the beginning use medicines that only reduce abscesses, leaving the patient to divine judgement, unless there is hope for salvation from danger, relief of suffering, and some sign of improvement. And so you must try to make the reduction as soon as possible in the early stages of the cause, before abscesses form.
And if the member returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that reduces abscesses and treat the plague with drying agents appropriate for the wounds.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and separate fragments are formed in the bone, then you must perform their stabilization and do what we have said in the treatment of simple injuries as described earlier in those sections.
And be careful and avoid any harm within your power according to the teachings that I have previously given you; for this is better for you in front of your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and truly beneficial to your body at the right time. Thus ends the perfect words of Albucasis, may God bless him. (This is a closing sentence often used in medieval texts to indicate the end of a work.) Indeed, amen. (This is a response often used in religious contexts to express agreement or confirmation of a prayer or blessing.)
